Two other books in the OUP VSI series that complement and expand on the current one are [3mMathematics [0mby the Fieldโs medallist 
Timothy Gowers and [3mCryptography [0mby Fred Piper and Sean Murphy. Probability and statistics, fields that were neglected here in [3m
Numbers [0m, are the subject of the [3mVSI Statistics [0mby David J. Hand. 

An insight into the nature of numbers can be read in David Flanneryโs book, [3mThe Square Root of 2: A Dialogue Concerning a Number 
and a Sequence [0m(Copernicus Books, 2006). This leisurely account is in the Socratic mode of a conversation between a teacher and 
pupil. [3mOne to Nine: The Inner Life of Numbers [0mby Andrew Hodges (Short Books, 2007) analyses the significance of the first nine 
digits in order. Actually it uses each number as an umbrella for examining certain fundamental aspects of the world and introduces 
the reader to all manner of deep ideas. This contrasts with Tony Crillyโs [3m50 Mathematical Ideas You Really Need To Know [0m(Quercus 
Publishing, 2007), which does as it says, digesting each of 50 notions into a four-page description in as straightforward a manner 
as possible. The explanations are mainly through example with a modest amount of algebraic manipulations involved, rounded off 
with historical details and timelines surrounding the commentary. A particularly nice account on matters concerned with binomial 
coefficients is the paperback of Martin Griffiths, [3mThe Backbone of Pascalโs Triangle [0m(UK Mathematics Trust, 2007), in which you 
will read proofs of Bertrandโs Postulate and Chebyshevโs Theorem, giving bounds for the number of primes less thanโโ. 

[3mElementary Number Theory [0mby G. and J. Jones (Springer-Verlag, 1998) gives a gentle but rigorous introduction and goes as far as 
aspects of the famous Riemann Zeta Function and Fermatโs Last Theorem. The classic book [3mAn Introduction to the Theory of Numbers [0m, 
by G. H. Hardy and E. M. Wright, 6th edn (Oxford University Press, 2008) assumes little particular mathematical knowledge but hits 
the ground running. The authorโs book [3mNumber Story: From Counting to Cryptography [0m(Copernicus Books, 2008) has more in the way of 
the history of numbers than this [3mVSI [0mand includes mathematical details in the final chapter. [3mThe Book of Numbers [0mby John Conway 
and Richard Guy (Springer-Verlag, 1996) is full of history, vivid pictures, and all manner of facts about numbers. Quite a lot of 
the history and mystery surrounding complex numbers is to be found in [3mAn Imaginary Tale: The Story of [0mโโโโโ(Princeton University 
Press, 1998) by Paul J. Nahin. Paul Halmosโs [3mNaive Set Theory [0m(Springer-Verlag, 1974) gives a quick mathematical introduction to 
infinite cardinal and ordinal numbers, which were not introduced here. 

A popular account of the Riemann Zeta Function is the book by Marcus du Sautoy, [3mThe Music of the Primes, Why an Unsolved Problem 
in Mathematics Matters [0m(HarperCollins, 2004), while Carl Sabbaghโs, [3mDr Riemannโs Zeros [0m(Atlantic Books, 2003) treats essentially 
the same topic. 

There are two accounts of the solution to Fermatโs Last Theorem, those being [3mFermatโs Last Theorem: Unlocking the Secret of an 
Ancient Mathematical Problem [0mby Amir D. Aczel (Penguin, 1996) and [3mFermatโs Last Theorem [0mby Simon Singh (Fourth Estate, 1999). The 
best popular book on the history of coding up to the RSA cipher is also an effort of Simon Singh: [3mThe Code Book [0m(Fourth Estate, 
2000). The unsolvability of the quintic (fifth-degree polynomial equations) was not explained in our text here but is the subject 
of an historical account: [3mAbelโs Proof: An Essay on the Sources and Meaning of Mathematical Unsolvability [0m(MIT Press, 2003) by 
Peter Pesic.

A very high-quality web page that allows you to dip into any mathematical topic, and is especially rich in number matters, is Eric 
Wolframโs [3mMathWorld: mathworld.wolfram.com [0m. For mathematical history topics, try [3mThe MacTutor History of Mathematics archive [0mat 
St Andrews University, Scotland: [3mhttps://www-history.mcs.st-andrews.ac.uk/history.index.html [0m. Web pages accessed 8 October 2010. 
Wikipediaโs treatment of mathematics by topic is generally serious and of good quality, although the degree of difficulty of the 
treatments is a little variable. For example, Wikipedia gives a good quick overview of important topics such as matrices and 
linear algebra.
